Topic 1 - THE LEGAL PROCESS 1.Â  COURTS (Tuesday, Aug. 28, 2012)Â  The state and federal legal systems of the United States.Â  The appellate court hierarchy: which courts are bound by which decisions?Â  How to read a legal opinion (a critical skill for the course). View SLIDES . Reading: Introduction to the Court System (Barclay) Appellate Review and Stare Decisis (JudgeÂ Kelsey) How to Read a Judicial Opinion: A Guide for New Law Students (Kerr) How to Read a Legal Citation Basic Legal Citation: Starting Points National A-1 Advertising, Inc. v. Network Solutions, Inc. 121 F. Supp. 2d 156 (D. N.H., 2000) Optional reading: A Guide to Legal Literacy (California State Bar) The Judicial System in North Carolina Teaching Stare Decisis Using Browsewrap Agreements (Sprague) Understanding the Federal Courts 2.Â  LAWSUITS (Thursday, Aug. 30, 2012)Â  The progress of a lawsuit from pre-filing through filing, discovery, motions, trial and appeals.Â  Litigation strategy and alternative dispute resolution: arbitration, mediation and settlement.Â  View SLIDES . Reading: Overview of the Civil Court Process (Indiana) Federal Rules of Civil Procedure (VERY LONG -- just skim for an overview) A Practical Guide to Patent Trial Discovery (Jack Griem) E-Discovery (Thompkins) Mediation; Alternative Dispute Resolution (ADR) (Alaska Judicial Council) ADR of Intellectual Property Disputes (McConnaughay) Optional reading: A Practical Introduction to Electronic Discovery (Judge Nuffer) Introduction to Discovery (Stratton Press) 3.Â  INTERPRETING STATUTES (Tuesday, Sept. 4, 2012) Technology advances rapidly, and statutes and legal decisions can't keep up.Â  This means that old laws are constantly being applied to situations not contemplated when the laws were originally passed.Â  This means that a court must interpret the words of a statute in a new context, a process called statutory interpretation.Â  This is not a haphazard process, but is guided by specific rules which, unfortunately, can produce anomalies that must be remedied later by the legislature.Â  We will look at interpretation of non-technical statutes in light of new technology.Â  View SLIDES . Reading: Statutory Construction Act, 19 Pa. C.S. 1921 Retail Ventures v. National Union Fire Insurance Company of Pittsburgh (6th Cir. Aug. 23, 2012) CRITICAL CASE, DECIDED TWO WEEKS AGO! AT&T v. City of Portland, 216 F.3d 871 (9th Cir. 2000) McBoyle v. U.S., 283 U.S. 25 (1931) (Supreme Court), McBoyle v. U.S., 43 F.2d 273 (10th Cir. 1930) People v. Bugaiski, 224 Mich. App. 241 (1997) This is a critical case for understanding the important principle of ejusdem generis. Rulings of the (VA) Tax Commissioner 03-2 U.S. v. Lacy, 119 F.3d 742 (9th Cir. 1997) Optional reading: Regina v. Ojibway (a classic of legal writing) New Hampshire v. MacMillan, 152 N.H. 67 (2005) Jarecki v. Searle, 367 U.S. 301 (1961) Joffe v. Acacia, 121 P.3d 831 (Ariz. Ct. App. Sept. 20, 2005) New Mexico v. Martinez, 34 P.3d 643, 131 N.M. 254 (2001) U.S. v. Pirello, 255 F.3d 728 (9th Cir. 2000) Grohs v. Florida, 2006 WL 3373103 (4th DCA 2006) N.Y. Times v. Tasini, 533 U.S. 483 (2001) (summary) Microsoft v. Commr of Internal Revenue, 311 F.3d 1178 (9th Cir. 2002) Statutory Interpretation: General Principles and Recent Trends (Congressional Research Service) (VERY LONG -- for reference only) 4.Â  JURISDICTION (Thursday, Sept. 6, 2012) The jurisdiction question is, "when does a court have the power to hear a particular case and bind the parties by its decision"?Â  Jurisdiction is often a key issue in determining whether a lawsuit is brought at all, and where and against whom it is brought.Â  Computer technology, particularly networking and wireless communication, has changed the way courts think about jurisdiction, which has historically been tied to physical presence in a particular state.Â  View SLIDES . Reading: Response Reward Systems v. Meijer, Inc., 189 F. Supp. 2d 1332 (M.D. Fla. 2002) Snowney v. Harrah's Entertainment, Inc., 34 Cal. 4th 1054, 112 P.2d 28 (2005) Optional Reading: Citron: Minimum Contacts in a Borderless World Geist: Is There a There There? Long-Arm Statutes (Vedder Price) (LONG -- FOR REFERENCE ONLY) 5.Â  INTERNET JURISDICTION (Tuesday, Sept. 11, 2012) The Internet has raised a host of new jurisdictional questions because packets follow unpredictable paths during transmission and might pass through multiple states without the knowledge or intent of the sender.Â  Does each of these states have jurisdiction in a crime or breach of contract occurs as a result of the transmission?Â  If not, which states should have jurisdiction and why?Â  View SLIDES . Reading: Butler v. Beer Across America, 83 F.Supp. 2d 1261 (N.D. Ala. 2000) Intercon, Inc. v. Bell Atlantic Internet Solutions, Inc ., 205 F.3d 1244 (10 th Cir., March 9, 2000) Twentieth Century Fox, Inc.. v. ICraveTV, 53 U.S.P.Q. 2d 1831 (W.D. Pa. 2000)Â  Read Complaint .Â  Read Injunction . Law of Computer Technology (17-562, 17-662, 17-762) FALL 2025 OFFICIAL COURSE WEB PAGE Tuesdays and ThursdaysÂ  8:00 - 9:20 a.m. Dougherty A302 In-Person Expectation Course Overview This course is both a survey of computer law and an examination of how courts, juries, and administrative agencies make decisions on issues involving computer technology.Â  It is also a survey of the most important and controversial topics in technology law today.Â  The material is divided into six primary subjects: 1. Legal process: how courts operate, differences between civil and criminal law, who has to obey the determination of a court, over whom a court can exercise power, and regulatory law. 2. Evidence: what has to be proven to a court and how it is done, rules of evidence, burdens of proof, expert testimony. 3. eBusiness Law: software licenses, clickwrap contracts, electronic transactions. 4. Personal Intrusions: social media, libel and defamation, data privacy, position monitoring. 5. Intellectual Property: Trade secrets and confidentiality agreements.Â  Copyright: fair use, software copyrights.Â  Patents: what is patentable, how patents are obtained, software patents, Internet patents. 6. Government Regulation: AI law and regulation, Net neutrality, antitrust principles, computer crime. No legal background is required or assumed.Â  This is not a law school course. Great effort is expended to keep the syllabus current based on breaking legal events.Â  Therefore, the content and ordering of lectures may vary somewhat as the course progresses. NOTE: It is possible that the instructor may be obliged to testify at one or more trials during the semester. Any missed lectures will be rescheduled or held remotely via Zoom. Which Course Should I Take? All three courses meet in the same place at the same time.Â  They differ only in course length, number of homwork assignments and number of credits awarded. 17-762 is a 12-unit, full-semester course intended for graduate students, particularly those in the MS in Artificial Intelligence and Innovation program and Societal Computing Ph.D. students. 17-562 is a 9-unit, full-semester course intended for undergraduates and graduate students outside of S3D. 17-662 is a 6-unit minicourse offered in Fall Mini 1, consisting of the first 14 lectures.Â  It is required for students in the Privacy Engineering program.Â  The requirement can also be satisfied with 17-562 or 17-762.Â  Almost all students who register for 17-662 eventually switch into 17-562 or 17-762.Â  However, except for MS in Artificial Intelligence and Innovation students, who must take 17-762, any student may register for any version of the course. Everyone in all three courses will take a take-home final examination.Â  The final exam counts for 30% of the grade.Â  There are no midterm exams. Class participation counts for 10%.Â  Homework counts for 60%.Â  There is one mini-homework 0 for everyone, then 4 more homeworks in 17-762, 3 in 17-562 and 2 in 17-662. Instructor Michael I. Shamos , Ph.D., J.D,, is Distinguished Career Professor in the School of Computer Science.Â  Dr. Shamos is an intellectual property attorney admitted to the Pennsylvania Bar and the Bar of the United States Patent and Trademark Office.Â  He has previously taught courses in Intellectual Capital, eCommerce Legal Environment, and Internet Law and Regulation for the Tepper School of Business, as well as courses in the Language Technologies Institute, Software and Sociental Systems, Computer Science, Mathematics and Statistics Departments.Â  He was Director of the MSIT in eBusiness Technology in the Institute for Software Research at Carnegie Mellon from 2004-2018. He is now Director of the M.S. in Artificial Intelligence and Innovation.Â  Dr. Shamos is a frequent expert witness in Internet, computer copyright, patent, electronic payment, and electronic voting cases. No textbook There is no textbook because the materials necessary for this course are very recent and have not yet found their way into textbooks.Â  All of the readings are available on the Internet, and will be posted approximately one month in advance. Readings are to be done BEFORE the associated lecture. PLEASE NOTE: readings for future lectures may change as the course progresses if warranted by significant legal decisions.Â  So read ahead, but not too far ahead. Advice on how to succeed in the course can be found in the Course Handbook . You may find the course glossary useful in understanding legal terminology and abbreviations. Administrative Information The course meets twice a week on Tuesdays and Thursday 8:00-9:20 a.m.in Dougherty Hall A302, possibly occasionally via Zoom. Students are encouraged to work together on the homework assignments. Past experience has shown that students do better when they work in teams. However, after discussing problems and solutions jointly, each student must prepare their own paper individually, without copying material from other people. The final exam will be a tak

 >" face="Arial" font="" size="2"><b>Course Overview</b></font></p>
18<p><font face="Arial" size="2">This course is both a survey of computer law and 
 >an examination of how courts, juries, and administrative agencies make decisions
 > on issues involving computer technology.Â  It is also a survey of the most impor
 >tant and controversial topics in technology law today.Â  The material is divided 
 >into six primary subjects: </font></p>
19<p><font face="Arial" size="2"><b>1. Legal process:</b> how courts operate, diff
 >erences between civil and criminal law, who has to obey the determination of a c
 >ourt, over whom a court can exercise power, and regulatory law.Â  <br/>
20<b>2. Evidence:</b> what has to be proven to a court and how it is done, rules o
 >f evidence, burdens of proof, expert testimony.Â  <br/>
21<b>3. eBusiness Law:</b> software licenses, clickwrap contracts, electronic tran
 >sactions. <br/>
22<b> 4. Personal Intrusions: </b>social media, libel and defamation, data privacy
 >, position monitoring.<br/>
23<b>5. Intellectual Property:</b> Trade secrets and confidentiality agreements.Â  
 >Copyright: fair use, software copyrights.Â  Patents: what is patentable, how pate
 >nts are obtained, software patents, Internet patents.Â  <br/>
24<b>6. Government Regulation:</b> Â AI law and regulation, Net neutrality, antitru
 >st principles, computer crime.</font></p>
25<font face="Arial" size="2">
26<p class="MsoNormal">No legal background is required or assumed.Â  This is not a 
 >law school course.</p>
27<p class="MsoNormal">Great effort is expended to keep the syllabus current based
 > on breaking legal events.Â  Therefore, the content and ordering of lectures may 
 >vary somewhat as the course progresses.</p>
28<p class="MsoNormal" style="font-weight: bold;">NOTE: It is possible that the in
 >structor may be
29obliged to testify at one or more trials during the semester. Any missed lecture
 >s will be rescheduled or held remotely via Zoom.</p>
30<p><font color="#ff0000">
31<b>Which Course Should I Take?</b></font></p>
32<p>All three courses meet in the same place at the same time.Â  They differ only 
 >in course length, number of homwork assignments and number of credits awarded.</
 >p>
33<p>17-762 is a 12-unit, full-semester course intended for
34graduate students, particularly those in the MS in Artificial Intelligence and I
 >nnovation 
35program and Societal Computing Ph.D. students.</p>
36<p>17-562 is a 9-unit, full-semester course intended for
37undergraduates and graduate students outside of S3D.</p>
38<p>17-662 is a 6-unit minicourse offered in Fall Mini 1,
39consisting of the first 14 lectures.Â  It is required for students in the Privacy
 > Engineering program.Â  The requirement can also be satisfied with 17-562 or 17-7
 >62.Â  Almost all students who register for 17-662 eventually switch into 17-562 o
 >r 17-762.Â  However, except for MS in Artificial Intelligence and Innovation stud
 >ents, who must take 17-762, any student may register for any version of the cour
 >se.</p>
40<p>Everyone in all three courses will take a take-home final examination.Â  The f
 >inal exam counts for 30% of the grade.Â  There are no midterm exams. Class partic
 >ipation counts for 10%.Â  Homework counts for 60%.Â  There is one mini-homework 0 
 >for everyone, then  4 more homeworks in 17-762, 3 in 17-562 and 2 in 17-662.</p>
 ></font>
41<p><b><font color="#ff0000" face="Arial" size="2">Instructor</font></b></p>

 >.Â  Dr. Shamos is an intellectual property attorney
44admitted to the Pennsylvania Bar and the Bar of the United States Patent and Tra
 >demark Office.Â  He has previously taught courses in Intellectual Capital, eComme
 >rce Legal Environment, and Internet Law and Regulation for the Tepper School of 
 >Business, as well as courses in the Language Technologies Institute, Software an
 >d Sociental Systems, Computer Science, Mathematics and Statistics Departments.Â  
 >He was Director of the MSIT in eBusiness Technology in the Institute for Softwar
 >e Research at Carnegie Mellon from 2004-2018. He is now Director
45of the  M.S. in Artificial Intelligence and Innovation.Â  Dr. Shamos is a frequen
 >t expert witness in Internet, computer copyright, patent, electronic payment, an
 >d electronic voting cases.</font></p>
46<p><b><font color="#ff0000" face="Arial" size="2">No textbook</font></b></p>
47<font face="Arial" size="2">
48<p>There is no textbook because the materials necessary for this course are very
 > recent and have not yet found their way into
49textbooks.Â  All of the readings are available on the Internet, and will be poste
 >d approximately one month in advance.Â  <b><font color="#ff0000">Readings are to 
 >be done BEFORE the associated lecture.</font></b>Â 
50PLEASE NOTE: readings for future lectures may change as the course progresses if
 > warranted by significant legal decisions.Â  So read ahead, but not too far ahead
 >.</p>
51      Advice on how to succeed in the course can be found in the Course <a href=
 >"http://euro.ecom.cmu.edu/program/law/08-732/handbook.pdf">Handbook</a>.

54<p><b><font color="#ff0000" face="Arial" size="2">Administrative Information</fo
 >nt></b></p>
55<p>The course meets twice a week on Tuesdays and Thursday 8:00-9:20 a.m.in Dough
 >erty Hall A302, possibly occasionally via Zoom. </p>
56<p>Students are encouraged to work together on the homework assignments. Past ex
 >perience has shown that students do better when they work in teams. However, aft
 >er discussing problems and solutions jointly, each student must prepare their ow
 >n paper individually, without copying material from other people.</p>
57<p>The final exam will be a take-home over a period of at least 24 hours, date t
 >o be determined. It will be open-book, open notes and open Internet. However, un
 >like with homework, collaboration with others is not permitted during the final 
 >exam.</p>

62<p><font color="#ff0000"><b>Grading Policy</b></font></p>
63<p>Written work will not be re-graded except for manifest error.Â  A "manifest er
 >ror" is one that is immediately apparent
64from the paper itself, such as failure to grade a question or an arithmetic erro
 >r in computing a score.</p>

99                  2.Â REGULATORY LAW</b></font>Â  (Thursday, Aug. 28, 2025) â€“ 
100                  Far more law is embodied in government regulations imposed by 
 >administrative agencies than appears in statutes.Â  This is particularly true in 
 >the privacy space.Â  When properly enacted, regulations have the same force of la
 >w as if they were enacted by a legislature, and are enforced by the agencies, wh
 >ich can impose fines and penalties for violations.Â  How regulations are made, th
 >eir legal effect, appeals from agency decisions, interplay between government ag
 >encies and the courts.</font>

132<p><font color="#0000ff" face="Arial" size="2"><b>
133                  3.Â INTERPRETING STATUTES</b></font>Â  (<font face="Arial" size=
 >"2">Tuesday,
134Sep. 2, 2025</font>) â€“ <font face="Arial" size="2">Technology advances
135rapidly, and statutes and
136legal decisions can't keep up.Â  This means that old laws are constantly being ap
 >plied to situations not contemplated when the laws were originally passed.Â  This
 > means that a court must interpret the words of a statute in a new context, a pr
 >ocess called statutory interpretation.Â  This is not a haphazard process, but is 
 >guided by specific rules which, unfortunately, can produce anomalies to be remed
 >ied later by the legislature.Â  We will look at interpretation of existing statut
 >es in light of new technology not contemplated when the statutes were passed.. T
 >HIS IS THE SINGLE MOST IMPORTANT LECTURE IN THE COURSE.</font></p>

174<b>4.Â JURISDICTION</b></font><font face="Arial" size="2"> (Thursday,
175Sep. 4, 2025)  â€“ The jurisdiction
176question is, "when does a court have the power to hear a particular
177case and bind the parties by its decision"?Â  Jurisdiction is often
178a key issue in determining whether a lawsuit is brought at all, and
179where and against whom it is brought.Â  Computer technology,
180particularly networking and wireless communication, has changed the way
181courts think about jurisdiction, which has historically been tied to
182physical presence in a particular state.</font></p>

304<p><font color="#0000ff" face="Arial" size="2"><b>
305                  7.Â  SCIENTIFIC EVIDENCE</b></font><font face="Arial" size="2">
 > (Tuesday, Sep. 16, 2025) â€“ Some trials involve
306opinion evidence.Â  For example, what was the cause of the Minneapolis
307bridge collapse?Â  Did the software substantially perform according
308to its manual?Â  Does this technological measure effectively
309control access to a copyrighted work or not?Â  These are scientific
310matters not within the skill of either the judge or jury, so must be
311proven through expert testimony.Â  Who is an expert?Â  How do
312they qualify?Â  What happens when experts disagree (they always do
313in a lawsuit)?Â  How can an expert be challenged?Â  When are
314scientific theories recognized by courts?</font></p>
315<font face="Arial" size="2">
316<font face="Arial" size="2">
317<p>Reading: <a href="http://euro.ecom.cmu.edu/program/law/08-732/Evidence/Dauber
 >t-Dow.pdf">Daubert
318        v. Merrell Dow, 509 U.S. 579 (1993)</a> (14 pp. -- the principal case
319        on expert qualifications)<br/>
320<a href="http://euro.ecom.cmu.edu/program/law/08-732/Evidence/FryeVUS.pdf">Frye

172        (Thursday, Sept.13, 2012)  All trials involving proving facts.Â  (A 
173        case in which facts are not disputed does not go to trial.Â  This was 
174        explained in Lecture 2.)Â  The rules of evidence define which methods 
175        can be used to prove facts at trial.Â  Some of these, such as the 
176        hearsay rule, are quite complex.Â  Others, which may appear simple, may 
177        have their meaning stretched when computers are involved.Â  For example, 
178        suppose you dispute that you clicked "I accept" on a license agreement f
>or

397</font>666<b>18. COPYRIGHT IN COMPUTER PROGRAMS</b></font> (Thursday, Oct. 30, 2025) â€“ Com
 >puter programs contain both human expression and utilitarian components; that is
 >, they do not merely express concepts but serve as instructions to real machines
 > to perform useful functions.Â  There has always been tension as to whether compu
 >ter programs ought to be copyrightable at all, since some believe that granting 
 >a copyright on a program is effectively conferring a long-term patent on technol
 >ogy that probably has a short lifetime.Â  This tension has given rise to a great 
 >deal of litigation and now almost every software copyright case filed meets with
 > a defense that the program was not copyrightable in the first place.Â  We will e
 >xplore the limits of what is copyrightable and what is not.Â  Abstraction-filtrat
 >ion-comparison.Â  The Lotus v. Borland and Lexmark cases. Are APIs copyrightable?
 > Oracle v. Google.</font></p>

399<p><font color="#0000FF"><b>13. FILE SHARING</b></font>668Infringement of Computer Software</a> (3 pp.)<br/>
400    (Tuesday, Oct. 9, 2012)  "File sharing" is one of the great misnomers in 
401        technology law.Â  "Sharing" implies allowing others to have or use what 
402        you own.Â  "File sharing" means providing others with material you do 
403        not own, which is illegal.Â  File sharing often involves breaking copy 
404        protection or encryption on files to allow them to be copied, a problem 
405        addressed by the Digital Millennium Copyright Act (17. U.S.C.
406        <font size="2">
407</font>1201ff), one of the most controversial of technological statutes.Â

431<p><font color="#0000FF"><b>14. COPYRIGHT IN COMPUTER PROGRAMS</b></font>
432    (Thursday, Oct. 11, 2012)  Computer programs contain both human expression 
433        and utilitarian components; that is, they do not merely express concepts
> but  
434        serve as instructions to real machines to perform useful functions.Â

>e

437        program is effectively conferring a long-term patent on technology that 
438        probably has a short lifetime.Â  This tension has given rise to a great 
439        deal of litigation and now almost every software copyright case filed me
>ets  
440        with a defense that the program was not copyrightable in the first place
>.Â

442        Abstraction-filtration-comparison.Â  The Lotus v. Borland and Lexmark 
443        cases.Â  </p>

> of

Topic 1 - THE LEGAL PROCESS 1.Â  COURTS (Tuesday, Aug. 28, 2012)Â  The state and federal legal systems of the United States.Â  The appellate court hierarchy: which courts are bound by which decisions?Â  How to read a legal opinion (a critical skill for the course). View SLIDES . Reading: Introduction to the Court System (Barclay) Appellate Review and Stare Decisis (JudgeÂ Kelsey) How to Read a Judicial Opinion: A Guide for New Law Students (Kerr) How to Read a Legal Citation Basic Legal Citation: Starting Points National A-1 Advertising, Inc. v. Network Solutions, Inc. 121 F. Supp. 2d 156 (D. N.H., 2000) Optional reading: A Guide to Legal Literacy (California State Bar) The Judicial System in North Carolina Teaching Stare Decisis Using Browsewrap Agreements (Sprague) Understanding the Federal Courts 2.Â  LAWSUITS (Thursday, Aug. 30, 2012)Â  The progress of a lawsuit from pre-filing through filing, discovery, motions, trial and appeals.Â  Litigation strategy and alternative dispute resolution: arbitration, mediation and settlement.Â  View SLIDES . Reading: Overview of the Civil Court Process (Indiana) Federal Rules of Civil Procedure (VERY LONG -- just skim for an overview) A Practical Guide to Patent Trial Discovery (Jack Griem) E-Discovery (Thompkins) Mediation; Alternative Dispute Resolution (ADR) (Alaska Judicial Council) ADR of Intellectual Property Disputes (McConnaughay) Optional reading: A Practical Introduction to Electronic Discovery (Judge Nuffer) Introduction to Discovery (Stratton Press) 3.Â  INTERPRETING STATUTES (Tuesday, Sept. 4, 2012) Technology advances rapidly, and statutes and legal decisions can't keep up.Â  This means that old laws are constantly being applied to situations not contemplated when the laws were originally passed.Â  This means that a court must interpret the words of a statute in a new context, a process called statutory interpretation.Â  This is not a haphazard process, but is guided by specific rules which, unfortunately, can produce anomalies that must be remedied later by the legislature.Â  We will look at interpretation of non-technical statutes in light of new technology.Â  View SLIDES . Reading: Statutory Construction Act, 19 Pa. C.S. 1921 Retail Ventures v. National Union Fire Insurance Company of Pittsburgh (6th Cir. Aug. 23, 2012) CRITICAL CASE, DECIDED TWO WEEKS AGO! AT&T v. City of Portland, 216 F.3d 871 (9th Cir. 2000) McBoyle v. U.S., 283 U.S. 25 (1931) (Supreme Court), McBoyle v. U.S., 43 F.2d 273 (10th Cir. 1930) People v. Bugaiski, 224 Mich. App. 241 (1997) This is a critical case for understanding the important principle of ejusdem generis. Rulings of the (VA) Tax Commissioner 03-2 U.S. v. Lacy, 119 F.3d 742 (9th Cir. 1997) Optional reading: Regina v. Ojibway (a classic of legal writing) New Hampshire v. MacMillan, 152 N.H. 67 (2005) Jarecki v. Searle, 367 U.S. 301 (1961) Joffe v. Acacia, 121 P.3d 831 (Ariz. Ct. App. Sept. 20, 2005) New Mexico v. Martinez, 34 P.3d 643, 131 N.M. 254 (2001) U.S. v. Pirello, 255 F.3d 728 (9th Cir. 2000) Grohs v. Florida, 2006 WL 3373103 (4th DCA 2006) N.Y. Times v. Tasini, 533 U.S. 483 (2001) (summary) Microsoft v. Commr of Internal Revenue, 311 F.3d 1178 (9th Cir. 2002) Statutory Interpretation: General Principles and Recent Trends (Congressional Research Service) (VERY LONG -- for reference only) 4.Â  JURISDICTION (Thursday, Sept. 6, 2012) The jurisdiction question is, "when does a court have the power to hear a particular case and bind the parties by its decision"?Â  Jurisdiction is often a key issue in determining whether a lawsuit is brought at all, and where and against whom it is brought.Â  Computer technology, particularly networking and wireless communication, has changed the way courts think about jurisdiction, which has historically been tied to physical presence in a particular state.Â  View SLIDES . Reading: Response Reward Systems v. Meijer, Inc., 189 F. Supp. 2d 1332 (M.D. Fla. 2002) Snowney v. Harrah's Entertainment, Inc., 34 Cal. 4th 1054, 112 P.2d 28 (2005) Optional Reading: Citron: Minimum Contacts in a Borderless World Geist: Is There a There There? Long-Arm Statutes (Vedder Price) (LONG -- FOR REFERENCE ONLY) 5.Â  INTERNET JURISDICTION (Tuesday, Sept. 11, 2012) The Internet has raised a host of new jurisdictional questions because packets follow unpredictable paths during transmission and might pass through multiple states without the knowledge or intent of the sender.Â  Does each of these states have jurisdiction in a crime or breach of contract occurs as a result of the transmission?Â  If not, which states should have jurisdiction and why?Â  View SLIDES . Reading: Butler v. Beer Across America, 83 F.Supp. 2d 1261 (N.D. Ala. 2000) Intercon, Inc. v. Bell Atlantic Internet Solutions, Inc ., 205 F.3d 1244 (10 th Cir., March 9, 2000) Twentieth Century Fox, Inc.. v. ICraveTV, 53 U.S.P.Q. 2d 1831 (W.D. Pa. 2000)Â  Read Complaint .Â  Read Injunction . Williams v. America OnLine, Inc., (2001 Mass. Super. No. 00-0962) Yahoo! Inc. v. La Ligue Contre Le Racisme et LAntisemitisme, 433 F.3d 1199, 1246 (9th Cir. 2006) , overruling the district court decision . Zippo Manufacturing v. Zippo Dot Com Inc., 952 F.Supp. 1119 (W.D. Pa. 1997) Optional Reading: NOTE: there is a lot of content here.Â  Pick one or two references if you want depth on this topic. Boone: Bullseye!Â  Why a Targeting Approach to Jurisdiction in the E-Commerce Context Makes Sense Internationally Gladstone: Determining Jurisdiction in Cyberspace Gray: Minimum Contacts in Cyberspace Moore: Cyberjurisdiction (VERY GOOD) Spencer: Jurisdiction and the Internet Wimmer & Pogoriler: International Jurisdiction and the Internet Topic 2 - EVIDENCE 6. COMPUTER EVIDENCE (Thursday, Sept.13, 2012) All trials involving proving facts.Â  (A case in which facts are not disputed does not go to trial.Â  This was explained in Lecture 2.)Â  The rules of evidence define which methods can be used to prove facts at trial.Â  Some of these, such as the hearsay rule, are quite complex.Â  Others, which may appear simple, may have their meaning stretched when computers are involved.Â  For example, suppose you dispute that you clicked "I accept" on a license agreement for an Internet download?Â  How does the company prove you did?Â  This lecture concentrates on the use of computer-based evidence in trials.Â  View SLIDES , Reading: Federal Rules of Evidence (Articles I, IV, VIII, IX, X) Kerr, Computer Records and the Federal Rules of Evidence Legal Status of Optical Disk and Electronic Imaging Systems Rostoker & Rines, Computer Jurisprudence Sutherlin v. State of Indiana, 784 N.E.2d 971 (Ind. Ct. App. 2003) Optional Reading: Chung & Byer, The Electronic Paper Trail State of Tennessee v, Drake (Tenn. Crim. App. June 6, 2005) State of Tennessee v. Farner, 66 S.W.3d 188 (Tenn. 2001) Wolfson, Electronic Fingerprints, Doing Away with the Conception of Computer-Generated Records as Hearsay 7.Â  SCIENTIFIC EVIDENCE (Tuesday, Sept. 18, 2012) Some trials involve opinions.Â  For example, what was the cause of the Minneapolis bridge collapse?Â  Did the software substantially perform according to its manual?Â  Does this technological measure effectively control access to a copyrighted work or not?Â  These are scientific matters not within the skill of either the judge or jury, so must be proven through expert testimony.Â  Who is an expert?Â  How do they qualify?Â  What happens when experts disagree (they always do in a lawsuit)?Â  How can an expert be challenged?Â  When are scientific theories recognized by courts? Reading: Daubert v. Merrell Dow, 509 U.S. 579 (1993) Frye v. United States, 293 F. 1013 (App. D.C. 1923) Clausen v. M/V New Carissa, 339 F.3d 1049, 1056 (9th Cir. 2003) Commonwealth v. Serge, 896 A.2d 1170 (Pa. 2006), cert. den. (2006) Mikes Train House v. Lionel, 472 F.3d 398 (6th Cir. 2006) Affidavit of Benjamin Edelman Optional Reading: Giannelli, Expert Qualifications & Testimony Kolar, Scientific and Other Expert Testimony People v. LeGrand, 196 Misc 2d 179 (Sup Ct, NY County 2002) Selbak, Digital Litigation Williamson & LaVecchia, Admissibility of Expert Testimony Topic 3 - INTELLECTUAL PROPERTY 8. DOMAIN NAMES (Thursday, Sept.20, 2012) Intellectual property overview.Â  How domain names are assigned and registered.Â  What happens when trademark owners have a dispute over the same name, e.g., someone registers heinz.biz and H. J. Heinz objects?Â  Who wins?Â  (I think you know the answer to that, but what are the rules that apply in less obvious situations?)Â  Domain name trickery: cybersquatting, metatagging, framing and typopiracy. Reading: Full Sail, Inc. v. Spevak (Case 6:03-cv-887-Orl-31JGG, M.D. Fla., 2003) Full Sail, Inc. v. TRADE SECRETS (Tuesday, Sept. 25, 2012) A good rule of thumb is that something is a trade secret if it is secret and relates to trade (really).Â  All fast-moving technological fields, particularly the computer field, are replete with trade secrets.Â  What methods are legitimate to discover a competitor's trade secret?Â  Reverse engineering?Â  What happens if improper methods (theft, bribery) are used?Â  When does a trade secret cease being a trade secret? Reading: Uniform Trade Secrets Act (short) Paramanandam v. Herrmann, 827 N.E. 2d 1173 (Ind. App. 2005) Jennings v. Election Canvassing Commission . Appellate decision (very short) ConnectU v. Facebook complaint Economic Espionage Act, 18 U.S.C. 1832 Optional Reading: Ford Motor Co. v. Lane, 67 F.Supp.2d 745 (E.D. Mich. 1999) Cross Media Marketing Corp. v. Marie Nixon , 06 Civ. 4228 (MBM) (S.D.N.Y., August 11, 2006) 10. CONFIDENTIALITY AGREEMENTS (Thursday, Sept. 27, 2012) Almost every company in the computer industry requires employees to sign confidentiality and non-competition agreements.Â  Exactly what can they require people to sign and what can be enforced in court?Â  Surprisingly, there are vast differences among the states concerning these contracts. Reading: HP Complaint Against Mark Hurd , filed Sept. 10, 2010 Mark Hurd's Separation Agreement from Hewlett-Packard California Reject Inevitable Disclosure Doctrine Schlage Lock Co. v. Whyte (Cal. COPYRIGHT (Tuesday, Oct. 2, 2012) Copyright is one of the hottest topics in computer law right now and will occupy us for two weeks.Â  This lecture deals generally with the rights of copyright owners and what is copyrightable and what is not, the policy behind copyright and the relationship between the cost of copying and the trend toward infringement. Reading: Feist Publications v. Rural Tel. Service Co., 499 U.S. 340 (1991) Morrissey v. Procter & Gamble, 379 F.2d 675 (1st Cir. 1967) New York Times Co. v. FAIR USE (Thursday, Oct. 4, 2012) What use can be made of the copyrighted work of others?Â  This is somewhat defined in the United States Code at 17 U.S.C. 107, but court decisions interpreting this section vary widely.Â  We'll talk about sampling, and the safe harbor for ISPs, the Google (YouTube) and Internet Archive cases. Reading: American Geophysical Union v. Texaco, 60 F3d 195 (2d Cir. 1994) Sony Corp. v. Universal City Studios, 464 U.S. 417 (1984) LiveNation Motor Sports, Inc. v. Davis, 81 USPQ 2d 1267 (N.D. Tex. 2006) Sony v. Connectix, 203 F.3d 596 (9th Cir. 2000) Use My Photo (NY Times, Oct. 1, 2007) Optional Reading: CoStar Group, Inc. v. LoopNet, Inc., 373 F.3d 544 (4th Cir. 2004) Perfect 10, Inc. v. Amazon.com, Inc., 487 F.3d 701 (9th Cir. 2007) Parker v. Google (3d Cir. 2007) 13. FILE SHARING (Tuesday, Oct. 9, 2012) "File sharing" is one of the great misnomers in technology law.Â  "Sharing" implies allowing others to have or use what you own.Â  "File sharing" means providing others with material you do not own, which is illegal.Â  File sharing often involves breaking copy protection or encryption on files to allow them to be copied, a problem addressed by the Digital Millennium Copyright Act (17. U.S.C. 1201ff), one of the most controversial of technological statutes.Â  We will look at Napster and Grokster in relationship to the goals of copyright, the recent RealDVD cases, jailbreaking and music industry enforcement. Reading: A&M Records v. Napster, 114 F.Supp.2d 896 (N.D. Cal. 2000) , affd 239 F.3d 1004 (9th Cir. 2001), affd after remand, 284 F.3d 1091 (9th Cir. 2002) MGM v. Grokster, 545 U.S. 913 (2005) From Sony to Grokster Digital Millennium Copyright Act (U.S. Copyright Office Summary) Universal City Studios et al. v. Reimerdes, 111 F.Supp.2d 194 (S.D.N.Y. 2000) , affd 273 F.3d 429 (2nd Cir. 2001) UMG Recordings, Inc. v. mp3.com, 92 F. Supp. 2d 349 (S.D.N.Y. 2000) Optional Reading: Digital Rights Management Digital Rights Management II Online Intellectual Property Cases Test Copyright, Free Speech Tension Online Policy Group v. Diebold, 337 F. Supp. 2d 1195 (N.D.Cal. September 30, 2004) 14. COPYRIGHT IN COMPUTER PROGRAMS (Thursday, Oct. 11, 2012) Computer programs contain both human expression and utilitarian components; that is, they do not merely express concepts but serve as instructions to real machines to perform useful functions.Â  There has always been tension as to whether computer programs ought to be copyrightable at all, since some believe that granting a copyright on a program is effectively conferring a long-term patent on technology that probably has a short lifetime.Â  This tension has given rise to a great deal of litigation and now almost every software copyright case filed meets with a defense that the program was not copyrightable in the first place.Â  We will explore the limits of what is copyrightable and what is not.Â  Abstraction-filtration-comparison.Â  The Lotus v. Borland and Lexmark cases. Reading: Copyright Infringement of Computer Software Computer Associates International, Inc. v. Altai, Inc., 982 F.2d 693 (2d Cir. 1992) Herbert Rosenthal Jewelry Corp. v. Kalpakian, 446 F.2d 738 (9th Cir. 1971) Lexmark Intl, Inc.. v. Static Control Components, 387 F.3d 522 (6th Cir. 2004) Lotus v. Borland, 49 F.3d 807 (1st Cir. 1995) Optional Reading: The "Abstraction, Filtration, Comparison Test" (Ladas & Perry LLP) Abstraction and filtration of GNU Sort TUESDAY, OCT. 16, 2012.Â  READING DAY -- NO CLASS 15. THE PATENT PROCESS (Thursday, Oct. 18, 2012) What is a patent?Â  The tests for patentability: novelty, usefulness and non-obviousness.Â  What is obvious and who decides?Â  The patent examining process.Â  What are the parts of a patent and what constitutes infringement? Reading: An Overview of the US Patent System Legal FAQ: Introduction to Patent Law , Reasoner & Morrow Patent Litigation for High Technology and Life Sciences Companies , Fenwick & West Loebner U.S. Patent 6,019,393 Yang et al. U.S. Patent 6,536,068 Optional Reading: The Admissibility and Utility of Expert Legal Testimony in Patent Litigation , Pollack Patent Law Principles and Strategies , Auerbach What Does Forum Shopping in the Eastern District of Texas Mean for Patent Reform? , Taylor KSR International v. Telelex, 127 S. Ct. 1727, (2007) (major Supreme Court case on obviousness) 16. SOFTWARE PATENTS (Tuesday, Oct. 23, 2012) A huge number of software patents are now being issues, at the rate of hundreds per week.Â  They have produced a great deal of litigation and consternation in the software industry.Â  We will look at what is patentable about software and algorithms and examine some the challenges being brought against software patents. Reading: IP v. Red Hat Complaint Software Patent Litigation , McDonald et al. Levergood et al. U.S. Patent 5,708,780 Henderson et al. U.S. Patent 5,072,412 Optional Reading: Patent Scope and Innovation in the Software Industry , Cohn & Lemley An Empirical Look at Software Patents , Bessen Public Hearing on Use of the Patent System to Protect Software-Related Inventions Emerging Claim Formats for Software Inventions 17. BUSINESS METHOD PATENTS (Thursday, Oct. 25, 2012) Until 1992, methods of doing business (e.g. conducting garage sales, offering discount coupons) were not considered patentable, regardless of novelty.Â  In 1998, the State Street Bank case made it clear that business methods can also be patented and are subject to the same requirements as other types of inventions.Â  The U.S. Supreme Court decision in Bilski v. Kappos has significantly altered the landscape for software and business method patents. The rise of electronic commerce has spawned new methods of conducting business, some of which appear to be obvious and unpatentable.Â  However, the Patent Office has issued numerous patents on ecommerce business methods, some of which we will examine closely. Reading: Bilski v. Kappos (Sup. Ct. 2010) Internet and E-Commerce Patents , Wright Business Method Patents, Innovation, and Policy , Hall Boes U.S. Patent 5,193,056 Hartman et al. U.S. Patent 5,960,411 (Amazon one-click) Johnson U.S. Patent 6,941,281 Optional Reading: Introduction to Patent Searching , Baillie Cybersource v. Retail Decisions (CAFC, August 16, 2011) Ultramercial v. Hulu (CAFC, September 15, 2011) Patent Reform Act of 2011 18. WHAT'S OBVIOUS AND HOW DO YOU PROVE IT? (Tuesday, Oct. 30, 2012) In 2007, in the case of KSR v. Teleflex , the U.S. Supreme Court laid down the standards that must be met to prove that an invention would have been obvious to those skilled in the art at the time it was made and therefore is unpatentable.Â  Such an analysis requires great care because (1) even though "obvious," the invention was novel when made; (2) the jury must be made to understand the mindset of a hypothetical non-existent being called "a person having ordinary skill in the art," who is presumed to have only ordinary skills but is also presumed to be familiar with everything invented in his field up to that time, and clearly such a person cannot exist; and (3) the analysis must wind the clock back to the moment just before the invention was made, which might be as many as 20 years earlier than the date of trial. Reading: To be supplied. Optional Reading: To be supplied. Topic 4 - BUSINESS TRANSACTIONS 19. SOFTWARE LICENSES (Thursday, Nov. 1, 2012) What can you do with a software you buy in a store or download from the Internet?Â  What are the conditions under which such software is provided?Â  What is the effect of clicking "I accept" on a license agreement that is too long to read?Â  What about freeware, shareware and open source software?Â  Are shrinkwrap, clickwrap and browsewrap agreements enforceable? Reading: ProCD v. Zeidenberg, 86 F.3d 1447 (7 th Cir. 1996) Specht v. Netscape Communications Corp., 306 F.3d 17 (2nd Cir.2002) Comb v. PayPal, 218 F. Supp. 2d 1165 (N.D. Cal. 2002) Affinity Internet v. Consolidated Credit, 920 So. 2d 1286 (Fla. Dist. Ct. App. 2006) The Enforceability of Shrinkwrap License Agreements On-Line and Off-Line, Hayes The GNU Public License Optional Reading: Fundamentals of Software Licensing, Classen Taking the Case: Is the GPL Enforceable?, Wacha 20. ELECTRONIC TRANSACTIONS (Tuesday, Nov. 6, 2012) Ordinary sales transactions in the brick and mortar world are governed by the Uniform Commercial Code, some form of which has been enacted in 49 states.Â  The country has been struggling, though, to develop a consistent statute that applies to electronic transactions, in which the traditional methods of identifying parties and inspecting goods are not available.Â  Two competing statutes are the Uniform Electronic Transactions Act (UETA) and the Uniform Computer Information Transactions Act (UCITA), which we will compare and contrast.Â  Reading: Uniform Electronic Transactions Act E-Sign Act Overview of the Uniform Computer Information Transactions Act (UCITA) Electronic Signatures and Records Under ESIGN, UETA and SPeRS Optional Reading: Legal Issues in Open Source and Free Software Distribution, Nimmer Finding Common Ground in the World of Electronic Contracts, Dickens 21. INTERNET TAXES (Thursday, Nov. 8, 2012) Generally, when you buy something that is shipped from another state into Pennsylvania you don't pay sales tax to either Pennsylvania or the state of origin.Â  (We'll look at exactly why this is so.)Â  Most Internet sales involve an interstate shipment, so the expansion of electronic commerce is depriving states of an increasing share of tax revenue.Â  To counteract this trend, various states are devising new taxes on Internet use, which threatens the development of electronic business.Â  Congress has stepped into the fray because of its power to regulate interstate commerce.Â  We will look at the current Internet tax situation in the United States.Â  This topic is more interesting than you might think. Reading: National Bellas Hess v. Dept of Revenue of Illinois, 386 U.S. 753 (1967) Quill Corp. v. North Dakota Tax Commr, 504 U.S. 298 (1992) Internet Tax Freedom Act St. Tammany Parish Tax Collector v. Barnesandnoble.com, et al., Civ. Act. No. 05-5695 (E.D. La., March 22, 2007) Optional Reading: Amazon Pushes Hard to Kill a Tax (NY Times, Sept. 5, 2011) Making the Internet Tax Freedom Act Permanent Could Lead to a Substantial Revenue Loss for States and Localities , Mazerov (lobbying document) Virginia Tax Commissioner Ruling 06-103 (2006) 22. TECHNOLOGICAL ANTITRUST (Tuesday, Nov. 13, 2012) Computer technology has engendered previously unheard-of methods of stifling competition, for example, by disabling a computer's ability to install or use a competitor's software.Â  Microsoft has been a pioneer and primary exponent of such techniques.Â  For example, Google has recently alleged that Microsoft Vista deliberately slows down Google desktop search to favor Microsoft Desktop Search.Â  Naturally such behavior has antitrust implications, so we will look at applicable antitrust law and cases involving purely automated actions, such as disabling competitors' software. Reading: Sherman Antitrust Act, 15 U.S.C. 1ff (1890) Clayton Antitrust Act, 15 U.S.C. 12ff (1914) Microsoft Antitrust Judgment (D.D.C. Nov. 12, 2002) Ticketmaster v. RMG Technologies (C.D. Cal. Oct. 16, 2007) Optional Reading: Unilateral Technology Suppression , Chin Department of Justice Antitrust Division Manual Topic 5 - PERSONAL INTRUSIONS 23. DATA PRIVACY (Thursday, Nov. 15, 2012) Privacy is well-covered in other COS courses, so we will just scratch the surface to interest you in further study of the subject.Â  Exactly what is data privacy and why do people want it?Â  We'll look at the patchwork of statutes around the country that emphasize various aspects of data privacy and then console ourselves over the lack of any coherent body of data privacy law in the United States.Â  We'll look at the recent identity theft red flag rules to see an attempt to cure some of the damaging consequences of privacy intrusions. Reading: Pisciotta v. Old National Bancorp , 49 F.3d 629, (7th Cir. Aug. 23, 2007) In re Subpoena Duces Tecum To America Online. Inc. Va. Cir. Ct., Fairfax Cty., Misc. Law No. 40570, (Feb. 7, 2000) America Online, Inc. v. Anonymous Publicly Traded Co ., 542 S.E.2d 377 (Va. 2001) McLaren v. Microsoft, No. 05-97-00824 (Tex. Ct. App. May 28, 1999) Tattered Cover, Inc. v. City of Thornton, 44 P.3d 1044 (Colo. 2002) T iberino v. Spokane County, P.3d 1104, 1108 (Wash. Ct. App. 2000) Optional Reading: Reno v. Condon, 528 U.S. 141 (2000) In re Verizon Internet Services Subpoena, 240 F.Supp.2d 24 (D.D.C. 2003) OGrady v. Superior Court of Santa Clara County; Apple Computer, Inc., Real Party in Interest, Ct. App. Calif., 6 th App. Dist. (May 26, 2006) 24. MONITORING (Thursday, Nov. 20, 2012) Sensors and surveillance cameras are everywhere.Â  Websites and cellphones are hacked with regularity.Â  Cellphones in particular provide a goldmine of information, particularly location data.Â  Both law enforcement organizations and private companies are collecting data surreptitiously at an unprecedented rate.Â  When is it legal to monitor someone's activity and when and how is it legal to use the results? Reading: To be supplied. Optional Reading: To be supplied. NO CLASS ON THANKSGIVING, NOVEMBER 22 25. DEFAMATION AND FREE SPEECH (Tuesday, Nov. 27, 2012) With websites, Twitter, Facebook and other social media, everyone is now a publisher and, for that matter, an instant publisher.Â  There has always been a tension between freedom of speech and one's right not to be defamed through publication of false information.Â  Because the Internet is a worldwide medium, it is possible to cause great damage in another country without leaving home, and, more important, without subjecting oneself to the jurisdiction of foreign courts.Â  Or is it?Â  Can imprudent social media posts be retracted so they cannot be used against the poster in the future?Â  Is it legal to collect a person's posts and archive them, hoping to use them again the poster in the future? Reading: To be supplied. Optional Reading: To be supplied. Topic 6 - COMPUTER CRIME 26. COMPUTER CRIME (Thursday, Nov. 29, 2012) Computers have provided unparalleled tools for the commission of crime and offer equally unparalleled methods of avoiding detection.Â  Encryption and steganography Because so many businesses are completely dependent on computers, servers have become a target for extortion attempts, competitive attacks, theft of trade secrets and hacking with a variety of objectives, some as simple as publicizing causes.Â  The U.S. has been slow to cope with advancements in computer crime because of a fundamental principle of criminal law: crime statutes are strictly construed.Â  Simply stated, this means that an act is not a crime unless a statute makes it explicitly criminal.Â  There are no "common law" computer crimes.Â  With legislatures slow to draft laws to keep pace with criminals, there is a continuing gap between what is legal and what should be illegal.Â  The first lecture will deal with computers as instruments of crime. Reading: Hageseth v. Superior Court of San Mateo County, Cal. App. (1 st Dist., May 21, 2007) People v. World Interactive Gaming (Sup. Ct. N.Y.Co., July 24, 1999) In re Reynoso, 477 F.3d 1117 (9 th Cir. 2007) Optional Reading: State Criminal Jurisdiction in Cyberspace: Is There a Sheriff on the Electronic Frontier?, Berg NOVEMBER 24.Â  THANKSGIVING BREAK.Â  NO CLASS. 27. Alkhabaz, 104 F.3d 1492 (6th Cir. 1997) Optional Reading: Council of Europe Convention on Cybercrime California Penal Code 646.9 Cyberstalking California Penal Code 653m (1999) No Threatening Email Act CAN-SPAM Act, 15 U.S.C. 7701 Identity Theft Act, 18 U.S.C. 1028

Law of Computer Technology (17-562, 17-662, 17-762) FALL 2025 OFFICIAL COURSE WEB PAGE Tuesdays and ThursdaysÂ  8:00 - 9:20 a.m. Dougherty A302 In-Person Expectation Course Overview This course is both a survey of computer law and an examination of how courts, juries, and administrative agencies make decisions on issues involving computer technology.Â  It is also a survey of the most important and controversial topics in technology law today.Â  The material is divided into six primary subjects: 1. Legal process: how courts operate, differences between civil and criminal law, who has to obey the determination of a court, over whom a court can exercise power, and regulatory law. 2. Evidence: what has to be proven to a court and how it is done, rules of evidence, burdens of proof, expert testimony. 3. eBusiness Law: software licenses, clickwrap contracts, electronic transactions. 4. Personal Intrusions: social media, libel and defamation, data privacy, position monitoring. 5. Intellectual Property: Trade secrets and confidentiality agreements.Â  Copyright: fair use, software copyrights.Â  Patents: what is patentable, how patents are obtained, software patents, Internet patents. 6. Government Regulation: AI law and regulation, Net neutrality, antitrust principles, computer crime. No legal background is required or assumed.Â  This is not a law school course. Great effort is expended to keep the syllabus current based on breaking legal events.Â  Therefore, the content and ordering of lectures may vary somewhat as the course progresses. NOTE: It is possible that the instructor may be obliged to testify at one or more trials during the semester. Any missed lectures will be rescheduled or held remotely via Zoom. Which Course Should I Take? All three courses meet in the same place at the same time.Â  They differ only in course length, number of homwork assignments and number of credits awarded. 17-762 is a 12-unit, full-semester course intended for graduate students, particularly those in the MS in Artificial Intelligence and Innovation program and Societal Computing Ph.D. students. 17-562 is a 9-unit, full-semester course intended for undergraduates and graduate students outside of S3D. 17-662 is a 6-unit minicourse offered in Fall Mini 1, consisting of the first 14 lectures.Â  It is required for students in the Privacy Engineering program.Â  The requirement can also be satisfied with 17-562 or 17-762.Â  Almost all students who register for 17-662 eventually switch into 17-562 or 17-762.Â  However, except for MS in Artificial Intelligence and Innovation students, who must take 17-762, any student may register for any version of the course. Everyone in all three courses will take a take-home final examination.Â  The final exam counts for 30% of the grade.Â  There are no midterm exams. Class participation counts for 10%.Â  Homework counts for 60%.Â  There is one mini-homework 0 for everyone, then 4 more homeworks in 17-762, 3 in 17-562 and 2 in 17-662. Instructor Michael I. Shamos , Ph.D., J.D,, is Distinguished Career Professor in the School of Computer Science.Â  Dr. Shamos is an intellectual property attorney admitted to the Pennsylvania Bar and the Bar of the United States Patent and Trademark Office.Â  He has previously taught courses in Intellectual Capital, eCommerce Legal Environment, and Internet Law and Regulation for the Tepper School of Business, as well as courses in the Language Technologies Institute, Software and Sociental Systems, Computer Science, Mathematics and Statistics Departments.Â  He was Director of the MSIT in eBusiness Technology in the Institute for Software Research at Carnegie Mellon from 2004-2018. He is now Director of the M.S. in Artificial Intelligence and Innovation.Â  Dr. Shamos is a frequent expert witness in Internet, computer copyright, patent, electronic payment, and electronic voting cases. No textbook There is no textbook because the materials necessary for this course are very recent and have not yet found their way into textbooks.Â  All of the readings are available on the Internet, and will be posted approximately one month in advance. Readings are to be done BEFORE the associated lecture. PLEASE NOTE: readings for future lectures may change as the course progresses if warranted by significant legal decisions.Â  So read ahead, but not too far ahead. Advice on how to succeed in the course can be found in the Course Handbook . You may find the course glossary useful in understanding legal terminology and abbreviations. Administrative Information The course meets twice a week on Tuesdays and Thursday 8:00-9:20 a.m.in Dougherty Hall A302, possibly occasionally via Zoom. Students are encouraged to work together on the homework assignments. Past experience has shown that students do better when they work in teams. However, after discussing problems and solutions jointly, each student must prepare their own paper individually, without copying material from other people. The final exam will be a take-home over a period of at least 24 hours, date to be determined. It will be open-book, open notes and open Internet. However, unlike with homework, collaboration with others is not permitted during the final exam. Instructor Availability To ask a question or schedule an appointment outside of class, please send email to shamos@cs.cmu.edu . Questions of general interest to the class should be asked in class, not in email or in a private meeting. Grading Policy Written work will not be re-graded except for manifest error.Â  A "manifest error" is one that is immediately apparent from the paper itself, such as failure to grade a question or an arithmetic error in computing a score. HOW TO GET AN "A" IN THIS COURSE Read and follow the advice in the Course Handbook . DEFAMATION, FREE SPEEECH AND SECTION 230 (Tuesday, Sept. 30, 2025) â€“ With websites, Twitter, Facebook and other social media, everyone is now a publisher and, for that matter, an instant publisher.Â This has given rise to fake news, doxing, revenge porn and other such acts. There has always been a tension between freedom of speech and one's right not to be defamed or embarrassed through publication of false information (or wrongful revealing of true information).Â  Under the Communications Decency Act (Section 230), when is a website liable for the positings of its subscribers? Because the Internet is a worldwide medium, it is possible to cause great damage in another country without leaving home, and, more important, without subjecting oneself to the jurisdiction of foreign courts.Â  Or is it?Â  Can imprudent social media posts be retracted so they cannot be used against the poster in the future?Â  Is it legal to collect a person's posts and archive them, hoping to use them again the poster in the future? Kentucky, Jan. 21, 2011) .Â  Appeal: Dirty World LLC v Jones, 755 F.3d 398 (6th Cir. 2014) NetChoice v. Paxton (5th Cir., 9/16/2022) Optional Reading: Aggergaard, Three Defenses to Consider Vogel v. COPYRIGHT IN COMPUTER PROGRAMS (Thursday, Oct. 30, 2025) â€“ Computer programs contain both human expression and utilitarian components; that is, they do not merely express concepts but serve as instructions to real machines to perform useful functions.Â  There has always been tension as to whether computer programs ought to be copyrightable at all, since some believe that granting a copyright on a program is effectively conferring a long-term patent on technology that probably has a short lifetime.Â  This tension has given rise to a great deal of litigation and now almost every software copyright case filed meets with a defense that the program was not copyrightable in the first place.Â  We will explore the limits of what is copyrightable and what is not.Â  Abstraction-filtration-comparison.Â  The Lotus v. Borland and Lexmark cases. Are APIs copyrightable? Oracle v. Google. Reading: Copyright Infringement of Computer Software (3 pp.) Computer Associates International, Inc. v. Altai, Inc., 982 F.2d 693 (2d Cir. 1992) (22 pp.) Lexmark Intâ€™l, Inc.. v. Static Control Components, 387 F.3d 522 (6th Cir. 2004) (32 pp.) Lotus v. Borland, 49 F.3d 807 (1st Cir. 1995) (20 pp.) Oracle v. Google,750 F.3d. 1339 (Fed. Cir. 2014) , (69 pp. -- skim for ideas. Controverisal ruling that APIs are copyrightable Optional Reading: The "Abstraction, Filtration, Comparison Test" (Ladas & Perry LLP) (4 pp.) Abstraction and filtration of GNU Sort (5 pp.) NOV. 4. COMPUTER CRIME (Thursday, Dec. 4, 2025) â€“ Computers have provided unparalleled tools for the commission of crime and offer equally unparalleled methods of avoiding detection. Because so many businesses are completely dependent on computers, servers have become a target for extortion attempts, competitive attacks, theft of trade secrets and hacking with a variety of objectives, some as simple as publicizing causes.Â  The U.S. has been slow to cope with advancements in computer crime because of a fundamental principle of criminal law: crime statutes are strictly construed.Â  Simply stated, this means that an act is not a crime unless a statute makes it explicitly criminal.Â  There are no "common law" computer crimes.Â  With legislatures slow to draft laws to keep pace with criminals, there is a continuing gap between what is legal and what should be illegal.Â  The first lecture will deal with computers as instruments of crime. Computer Fraud and Abuse Act, crimes against computer systems: denial of service attacks, vandalism, cyberterrorism. Reading: Computer Fraud and Abuse Act (6 pp.) Pennsylvania Unlawful Use of Computer Statute, 39 Pa.C.S. Â§ 3933 (3 pp.) Van Buren v. United States, 593 U.S.--- (2021) (37 pp.) In re Reynoso, 477 F.3d 1117 (9 th Cir. 2007) (15 pp.) People v. World Interactive Gaming (Sup. Ct. N.Y.Co., July 24, 1999) (8 pp.) Optional Reading: Hageseth v. Superior Court of San Mateo County, Cal. App. (1st Dist., May 21, 2007) (37 pp., unusual case in which a court is sued) First Do No Harm: The Problem of Spyware, Crawford (43 pp., legal status of spyware) Prosecuting Computer Crimes (213 pp., Department of Justice manual) State Criminal Jurisdiction in Cyberspace: Is There a Sheriff on the Electronic Frontier? (7 pp., Berg) Indictment of J. John Ancheta under the Computer Fraud and Abuse Act (52 pp.) DECEMBER 8-9 TAKE-HOME FINAL.
